    <li> Did the job fantastically for the first 6 months then it gradually started to die. After 11 months I read it it&#x27;s last rites and chunked it in the garbage can .     <li> this is a replacement, wouldnt be without it, probably the only choice when it comes to waterpik u can use in the shower.....</li>
    <li> My dentist gave me this Waterpik -- a wonderful gesture, to help me care for my hard-to-floss teeth.  I charged the battery up, and for the first week, it was a great tool for dental hygiene.  Super clean teeth, no kidding.      I followed the instructions to the letter regarding the care and feeding of this device, but within 2 weeks (after the second time I charged it up), it stopped charging when I plugged it in -- wouldn&#x27;t charge at all.  I mean, literally.    Since it stopped working so quickly, I contacted WaterPik and told them the problem.  I had noticed  what I perceived to be a design flaw:  The little rubber flap that covers the opening where you plug the charger into the device opens from the top.  Perhaps if it opened from the bottom, water wouldn&#x27;t get in it so easily and corrode the poor thing.  But corroded it was, half a month after I opened the brand-new package (When I mentioned this to the fine folks at Waterpik, they told me it was a great design, that water couldn&#x27;t corrode it, and that it was evidently my thinking that was flawed).  For some reason, their reasoning thus left me feeling insulted.    They were willing to send me a free replacement -- all I needed to do was send them my original receipt.    I explained (again, since I mentioned it in my original communication with them) that it was a gift from my dentist; I did not have the receipt.    They responded, telling me I needed to tell the dentist to give me the original receipt.  Excuse me??  Were they kidding??  TELL him to give it to me?    I explained to them I&#x27;m not in the habit of asking my doctors and dentists to open up their accounts payable for my review or ask them to show me paid receipts for what they buy.  I said I was willing to send the device back to them, with the original box, instructions etc. so I could prove I wasn&#x27;t just trying to get something for nothing but just wanted what I believed to be a defective product replaced.    After hemming and hawing for about a month they finally agreed to send me a replacement.  They didn&#x27;t want me to return the defective one, they said.  And 2 weeks later, I did receive a replacement.  It came with a clear caveat:  Since this one replaced a defective one, there was NO GUARANTEE OR WARRANTY for the product.    I charged the new one up, and it worked fine for about a month. Well, guess what.... yep, it won&#x27;t hold a charge.  It&#x27;s not a quality product, and that&#x27;s a fact.    When I first used the first one, I liked it so much I was ready to run out and buy some to give as gifts to family and friends.... boy am I glad I didn&#x27;t!  I&#x27;ve gone through enough headaches with this piece of garbage that I&#x27;ll never buy one.  I&#x27;ll use a plug-in Waterpic model, or another manufacturer&#x27;s product. (Maybe somebody out there makes a cordless rechargeable oral irrigation/floss system that doesn&#x27;t fail so quickly?)</li>
    <li> This is a little awkward to use, I feel the design could be better but it is amazingly effective - even after brushing and flossing.  I recommend it to anyone who has had gum problems.</li>
    <li> A great product.  Have been using this for years.</li>
    <li> I purchased this item without reading the previous reviews... MISTAKE, MISTAKE, MISTAKE.    As others reported using different words, this cordless device is a RIP-off, ergo Waterpik RIP.  Don&#x27;t waste your money.  This device is only good if you have no teeth because the pressure is inferior except to get the gingiva wet with a pressure at an ineffective, worthless level.    The corded model, which is older than the cordless model I have been using, works well.  Too bad there is no ZERO star rating; better yet a minus star rating.</li>
    <li> I bought a different model waterpik and have been very disappointed with the device&#x27;s tips quality.  The tips are extremely fragile. if the device tips over, they WILL break.  Mine broke within 2 days.  I contacted Customer Service and was told they would ship me a new set of tips, that was in December 2010, and as of today, Feb 13, 2011, I have not received the replacement tips. I will never buy another waterpik product.</li>
    <li> The punch line first:  get the electric model rather than the cordless rechargeable.  I bought the WP-360W and really had no complaints the first month -- I&#x27;d charge it,  use it a couple days,  then recharge it.  After a month it was on the charger constantly.  After 4 months,  it died completely.  I called Waterpik and they were willing to replace it but I elected to \\&quot;upgrade\\&quot; to the electric model---and I&#x27;m really really glad I did.     I&#x27;m all for portable and cordless ( Phones,  computer, ipod, etc.);  but sometimes electric just works a lot better than battery---- and power washers,  air compressors ,  and waterpiks are examples.  I&#x27;m sure there are a few of you  that are on the road 5 days a week and don&#x27;t want to lug the electric model around with you.  But if your buying for home use,  it would be hard to convince me the electric isn&#x27;t the smart buy.  After 4 months,  the electric is still working with more force than the cordless had initially.  The hand unit with nozzle is smaller and easier to maneuver around the mouth than the cordless with the water container attached. The electic is easier to keep clean --my opinion.   The power cord and water tube to the handle are plenty long to get your mouth over the sink as long as you have at least one outlet near your sink / vanity.  ( If you have a pedestal sink then you may have a problem)  The water tank is plenty big to waterpik the front and back of all teeth a couple times.     One star for the cordless,  5 stars for the electric</li>
    <li> This is a well designed product that works well to clean between teeth.   Compared to other similar products I have had this one is small which encourages use.   I took off one star because I have not been able to keep the top part stationary in my mouth.  It still works fine, though</li>

    <li> What we got here is a bimodal distribution!&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;People either sing the praises of the \\&quot;Water Jet,\\&quot; or wish to relegate it to the dung heap of poorly designed consumables.  Who&#x27;s right? While there&#x27;s no one answer to that, here are some things you might want to consider:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Pros:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1. Portability. The unit is rechargeable, and doesn&#x27;t use a cord, thus increasing its portability and ease of use. It&#x27;s also much smaller and more discreet than the traditional water piks that work with a reservoir.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;2. Professional recommendations. Dentists and dental hygienists recommend it, and it apparently does improve oral hygiene.  It removes things that brushing often leaves behind.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;3. Two types of heads.  Here, two heads are better than one.  Water pik varies the diameter of the flow-thru attachment to achieve either gentle or not-as-gentle pressure.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;4. Price.  Definitely a good deal, especially on sale, and compared to similar products.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cons:&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1. The MOST frequent complaint here is that the unit eventaully won;t recharge, and that happened to me as well.  However, this was not after 3 weeks or 3 months, but after about a year of use. That&#x27;s annoying, but what&#x27;s even more annoying is that the \\&quot;water jet\\&quot; doesn&#x27;t HOLD a charge for very long. After just a few uses, you&#x27;ll need to plug it in again.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;ANother frequent complaint is that the internal reservoir is so small you have to fill it several times to clean your entire mouth.  Again, this was my experience, but (again), it was not as bad as what some people have written.  If I hurried, I could do it in one filling, but for a fairly thorough job, it does take two.  Not terrible, as long as it keeps working (see paragraph above).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;2. Professional recommendation.  When this first came out, it had great appeal as a portable and easy-to-store device that cleaned--floss-like--in difficult areas around the gums.  However, it&#x27;s now apparent there are some longterm problems, especially with the batteries.  Now, competition looms in the form of a similar (non-rechargeable) unit from Panasonic.  If costs more, but if it produces similar results without the problems, I&#x27;m pretty sure dentists will recommend it as well (or instead).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;3. Two heads.  NOt a bad idea, although many people will find that they use only the higher pressure nozzle.  However, if your gums are sensitive, you may want to work your way up from lower to higher.  Basically, a good idea, especially since the price is so low.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;4. Price.  On sale for about $20, this is potentially a very cost efficient way to reduce your dental bills.  However, one must balance that against the many reviewers who have registered complaints about the batteries, etc.  The Panasonice is about 50% more expensive, and has the same small reservoir problem.  However, if it proves more reliable, this will be the one to get.  And that&#x27;s my recommendation: Look at the competition (the Panasonic), and see what kind of reviews it gets.  ALso, consider whether it would really be so bad (and, practically speaking, if you have the room) to have the traditional style water jet (whatever the brand) which plugs into the wall and has an external reservoir.it&#x27;s now apparent there are some longterm problems, especially with the batteries.  Now, competition looms in the form of a similar (non-rechargeable) unit from Panasonic.  If costs more, but if it produces similar results without the problems, I&#x27;m pretty sure dentists will recommend it as well (or instead). &lt;br /&gt; &lt;br /&gt;3. Two heads.     <li> If you are addicted to using waterpicks this may be useful:&lt;br /&gt;On a long bike tour my travel waterpick lost its battery power to the point that it really did not work to clean my teeth, and I could not find a similar product in the countries I was travelling in.  So eventually I replaced the chargeable battery pack (which is basically two rechargeble AA batteries taped together) with two non-rechargeble AA batteries.  I have done this twice now.  It works like new for about 10 days, then the batteries die again.  Of course, I could no longer plug it in. But this solution worked long enough for me to get home to my stationary waterpick.&lt;br /&gt;So if you really HAVE to, pop the reservoir off with a flathead screw driver.  Use a philips screwdriver to remove the two screws in the base of the machine, use the flathead again to pry the guts out from the plastic case.  remove the battery-pack and create your own with two AAs, tape the appropriate wires to the appropriate battery contact points. And one important detail- on the battery pack there is a metal strip that connects the two contact points that are not connected to wires- save that metal strip and tape it to your home-made battery pack&#x27;s contact points which are opposite of the wires. Test if it turns on before you put it all back together again.</li>
    <li> I have had this product for over a year and wish I had gotten it sooner.  Works great for dental care. AAA+++</li>
    <li> So far, I have bought three of these and the life expectancy is about a year before they die. I liked the small model, but am going to have to find another model as they just don&#x27;t last and I feel are a waste of money at this point.</li>

Reading the other reviews I wonder if I just got a good version of the product.  Here&#x27;s a couple suggestions for those of you who buy it.  1) Keep the little plastic plug that covers the recharging port closed so water does not get in it, and corrode it.  2) The top plastic tip rotates so you can easily get your back teeth.  3) I have noticed with most rechargeable items that if you leave them plugged in once they are charged it seems to damage the battery, ie it doesn&#x27;t hold the charge as long. I only leave my plugged in to charge for about an hour every couple days.  4) For a full cleaning you will need to refill the tank once. I use this before I floss to get rid of any large particles stuck in my teeth.   5) The on/off button and the plastic tip release are kind of close to one another at the top so just learn where they are so you don&#x27;t hit the wrong one and have water go all over the place.</li>

    <li> A wall-powered (not a rechargeable battery) Waterpik does a very good job of flushing out food between my teeth. In some hotel rooms, the power outlets do not make good connection, and one needs to wiggle the power brick to get a good connection. Without a power indicator, there is no way to tell if the unit is charging. A light to indicate when the unit is fully charged would be handy.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It has not happened as yet, but then I&#x27;ve only been using the unit for a week, but I fully expect to the connection of the battery charger to the unit to quickly deteriorate.  If (perhaps when) water gets in around the two pins, there will be corrosion because a small voltage appears to be present across the pins at all times.  This small voltage is present even when the charger is not plugged into the unit.  As a minimum, the unit should be provided with a rubberize plug to keep the connector dry when the unit is in use.  A better approach would be a magnetically coupled charger like that used for the Braun electric toothbrushes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;My unit is only a week old, and already the motor sounds like it is struggling due to an inadequately charged battery. The only positive is that Waterpic has promised to replace it free of charge.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The water tank needs to be either made from clear plastic or have a clear stripe down the side, so one can tell when it is full. Without same, the only way to quickly fill it is to pour water into it until it overflows.  Of course, when it overflows, the connector area is likely to get wet.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The length of the unit combined with the low faucet height in most hotel sinks makes it difficult to fill the unit without spilling water. Again, when it overflows, the connector area is likely to get wet. The fill cap needs to be redesigned to allow quick filling from a low faucet.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Bottom line, I will not be surprised that it no longer works when I go to take it on my next trip in a few weeks.     <li> I purchased the Waterpik WB-360 in September 2009. At first, I enjoyed the convenience of the cordless device, not being tethered to a wall plug. The small water capacity and need to refill at times during use, which other reviewers have also mentioned, was a limitation but was tolerable. However, within about five months, a number of problems started to appear. The motor would stop working (no sound and no water) during use, or the water flow and the sound of the motor would become weak, or the motor would sound like it was on but no water would come out. At first, these problems were intermittent, and turning off and on several times would restore function, but finally it just stopped working altogether. I called Waterpik and they were very gracious. agreeing to send me a new unit based on trust, without even sending back the non-functioning unit. However, their customer support seemed clueless about the problem. They justified sending a new unit based on \\&quot;inability to hold a charge,\\&quot; which could not have been the cause since recharging it had little or no effect, and during the early stages it went off and back on at full power without recharging. The problems are more likely a physical blockage from debris getting into the water circulation, and/or an electrical short.    If only one unit had been bad and the replacement unit had worked fine, I would not be writing this review. But after a few months, the same exact problem occurred with the new one they had sent me as a replacement. I understand that there has been some minor modification in the product since this occurred, but the company has not given any public indication that this was to correct the type of problems described above. I therefore recommend that nobody purchase this product. However, I do think Waterpik is a good company in general and there is no comparable line of products that I am aware of, so I have purchased the Waterpik WP-100, a larger, plug-in unit, and have had no problems so far.</li>

    <li> As a dental Hygienist I highly recommend this product and have seen some amazing reductions in patient&#x27;s bleeding gums (bleeding&#x3D;disease/infection). It is great for people with periodontal disease and gingivitis (over 90% of us have some-even professionals), as well as people who have large hands and people who have braces. I have even sold the product out of several offices because I love it so much. Here are some things to note.    1) It will take a little bit to get used to. I warn my patient&#x27;s that they may get water in their eyes the first few times. The resevoir is smaller and will need to be refilled.    2) It is very convenient. When you see this in your shower you are more likely to use it daily and make it a habit. You may have to recharge it every week or so when it runs down. Also, before you use it the first time fully charge it overnight.    3) You should keep the unit away from the running water when not in use. I have had two units and never had one corrode. You need to make sure the flap where you charge it is closed fully. Just use common sense...turn the charging portal away from the water when you refill, etc. I refill right in my shower.    4) The first one I had lasted a year and a half. These things do not last forever, but for the price they are so worth it. Most people pay more for coffee a month. Is your health worth that? Also Waterpik invented the machines and are very good about replacing any defective item. Every once in awhile you get a dud battery, I don&#x27;t care what kind of product it is. Call them and they will replace it.          For all of you who don&#x27;t know it.....we have a saying in dentistry that you only have to floss the ones you want to keep. Brushing alone will not cut it. If you do not clean under your gums you will have infection (bleeding) and bone loss around your teeth. The infection gets worse and deeper and will travel into your bloodstream. If the infection gets into your bloodstream it effects your whole body.If you have deeper pockets (probing depths) that measure 4 and above,  even if you are flossing you may not be cleaning to the base of the pocket where the really nasty bacteria is hiding out. This will get where your floss will not. It does not replace floss and I tell my patient&#x27;s to use them together. If you don&#x27;t know what your readings are ask your Dental Hygienist. They should be checking your pockets every visit and recording them once a year. You should see your hygienist 2-4 times a year.              Periodontal disease has been linked to Heart disease, Stroke, Diabetes, Alzheimer&#x27;s, Pre-term low birthweight babies, Prostate Cancer....the list goes on. If your body is constantly fighting an infection in your mouth your immune system cannot do it&#x27;s job if you get a cold, a cut....you name it.             I am getting off my soap box. I love this product. I was skeptical at first but got deperate with a few hopeless patient&#x27;s and saw amazing results...and started using it myself. Also, it will not remove hard deposits if you haven&#x27;t been cleaned recently, you have to have a clean mouth to begin with.        It is like anything else....you have to use it daily to see results. I use it (even before I got braces). I have my parent&#x27;s using one because I believe in the product. My dad has the big countertop model and loves it...though it costs more. I have been using my second unit for over a year now. I buy them for my friends and they love them. I would not spend money on them if they didn&#x27;t work.    Good luck and good health! Hope this info helped some people.</li>

    <li> The product works well, and ours actually lasted for several years.  Only the battery failed on us.&lt;br /&gt;* After 5 years, our drive gear shows very little wear, only the faintest hint of darkening of the silicone grease.&lt;br /&gt;* The little power brick is rated 100ma, 3V, but I found it to be 3.7V.  I didn&#x27;t verify BEFORE it failed; however, it&#x27;s a transformer, rectifier, and a filter cap.  If something failed internally, I&#x27;d expect ripple to go up, or voltage to go down, or squealing from a transformer short.  None of that seems to have happened.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The way to keep the battery alive for so long is to NOT keep it plugged in.  The \\&quot;memory effect\\&quot; of NiCd batteries can be at play; however, you can \\&quot;recondition\\&quot; them by using them down to where the motor stops, then immediately recharging it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Because it&#x27;s a NiCd battery pack, you must NOT leave this on the charger.  You want to charge it up for (overnight) and then leave it unplugged.  Use it until it doesn&#x27;t have the power to clean anymore, then recharge it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This isn&#x27;t a smart device -- there is no trickle charge, nor battery conditioner.  Overcharging a NiCd causes venting of hydrogen and oxygen from the breakdown of the water in its electrolyte.  It&#x27;s a one-way valve.  Once the battery is dried out, that&#x27;s it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Also, don&#x27;t leave it \\&quot;on\\&quot; with the battery dead.  Because this has 2 cells in series, one cell will always be slightly lower power than the other.  Once it goes below 2V, the weaker of the 2 cells will reverse polarity.  If it&#x27;s left in this condition, the other cell will reverse-charge it, causing it to off-gas its electrolyte, killing that cell completely.  If that happens, you can&#x27;t recharge it, and trying will make the battery pack get hot.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The battery is P/N 37059-7, the same as for the WP350.  Amazon sells a replacement as [[ASIN:B000V2DN24 Battery for WaterPik 350W travel model.]]  It&#x27;s more than half the cost of this unit (makes sense once you open it up) and requires soldering.  Probably not worth the cost.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The battery is made of two 4/5 sub-C cells with solder tabs.  It&#x27;s just like a C cell, but 4/5 as long, and the + nub doesn&#x27;t stick out as fer.  It&#x27;s NiCd 1000mAh at 2.4V.  You can pick up two 2200mAh 4/5 Sub-C cells with solder tabs for about 1/5th the cost of the Waterpik unit.  Once the 2 cells are connected, you can just wrap it in clear tape unless you happen to have heat-shrink tubing handy.  Surgery is about 5 minutes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;NOTE: You can&#x27;t throw these away legally because of the amount of cadmium in the battery pack.  It&#x27;s toxic and leaches into the water supply.  Don&#x27;t be lazy.  This needs to be taken to your local battery recycling shop.  If you can pull the cells out, that&#x27;s even nicer, but not really necessary.  Most hardware stores and battery stores will take returns for recycling.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The unit itself is fairly cheap, so if you treat it right and get 2-5 years out of it, then great.  If you&#x27;re prone to leaving things plugged in all the time, then you&#x27;ll definitely feel cheated on this.orth the cost.    The battery is made of two 4/5 sub-C cells with solder tabs.  It&#x27;s just like a C cell, but 4/5 as long, and the + nub doesn&#x27;t stick out as fer.  It&#x27;s NiCd 1000mAh at 2.4V.  You can pick up two 2200mAh 4/5 Sub-C cells with solder tabs for about 1/5th the cost of the Waterpik unit.  Once the 2 cells are connected, you can just wrap it in clear tape unless you happen to have heat-shrink tubing handy.  Surgery is about 5 minutes.    NOTE: You can&#x27;t throw these away legally because of the amount of cadmium in the battery pack.  It&#x27;s toxic and leaches into the water supply.  Don&#x27;t be lazy.  This needs to be taken to your local battery recycling shop.  If you can pull the cells out, that&#x27;s even nicer, but not really necessary.  Most hardware stores and battery stores will take returns for recycling.    The unit itself is fairly cheap, so if you treat it right and get 2-5 years out of it, then great.  If you&#x27;re prone to leaving things plugged in all the time, then you&#x27;ll definitely feel cheated on this.</li>

    <li> I&#x27;m old enough to be on Social Security &amp; have never, never, before written a negative review about anything.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This is the second unit I bought where the battery / charger / switch system deteriorated in about 16 months. The unit contains a flexible, bi-shape (one shape on -- one shape off), stamped metal switch that seems to either wear or loose its precise bi-shape memory. The result is that either the switch does not make sufficient contact to deliver sufficient power to the motor, or it always maintains a slight contact that drains &amp; ages the battery. Manually reshaping this \\&quot;switch\\&quot; restores proper function for a short time. But, such cold reshaping further deteriorates the metal&#x27;s shape memory and the same type of failure follows sooner &amp; sooner as additional fixes are applied.</li>

    <li> I am a regular user of a countertop Waterpik, Model WP-60W.  I use the Cordless  Model WP-360W only when traveling, because of its greater portability.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The WP-60W has adequate water pressure, not nearly as much as the countertop model, but it is a pulsating pressure, which I find important.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The water tank on the WP-60W is small, and requires 2 - 3 refills during a session.  The rechargeable battery on my unit permitted approximately 10 refills before needing recharging.  Although the tip rotates, the entire unit must be manipulated to achieve the desired angle against ones teeth.  Occasionally, food particles would find their way to the refill cap and care needed to be taken to rinse them away to prevent their entry into the tank.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;During my first use of the unit, the motor and pumping action stopped completely.  Close investigation revealed a small speck of dirt clogging the tip, which was cleared using a small wire.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Previous models of this product were subject to corrosion of the recharging terminals at the base of the unit.  While the terminals are still at the base, they are more visible, allowing for easier observation, prevention, and clearing of possible corrosion.</li>
